  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2584886164" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2584886164" />
    <a title="Jake Oelrichs" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/62695461-jake-oelrichs"><img alt="Jake Oelrichs" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1482179794p2/62695461.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2584886164?book_show_action=true">Nov 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jake Oelrichs" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jake Oelrichs" href="/user/show/62695461-jake-oelrichs">Jake Oelrichs</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/62695461-jake-oelrichs?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2584886164" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2872713770913471492">This book was my introduction to Ondaatje. When I read it in my early 20’s I was enthralled as if by the appearance of a new and beastly species of animal. I was amazed by its experimental form, poetic sparks, and shockingly brutal imagery. <br/> <br/>I’m 40 now, and was very curious to see how Coming Through Slaughter had aged for me. After a recent third reading, I gotta say, it remains one of my all-time favourite novels. Though, I have to admit, I’m now a little bit allergic to some of its dramatic ex</span>
  <span id="freeText2872713770913471492" style="display:none">This book was my introduction to Ondaatje. When I read it in my early 20’s I was enthralled as if by the appearance of a new and beastly species of animal. I was amazed by its experimental form, poetic sparks, and shockingly brutal imagery. <br /> <br />I’m 40 now, and was very curious to see how Coming Through Slaughter had aged for me. After a recent third reading, I gotta say, it remains one of my all-time favourite novels. Though, I have to admit, I’m now a little bit allergic to some of its dramatic excesses. I like my drama understatedly sizzling nowadays, not in your face, or straining credibility. Specifically, I could no longer get on board with the climactic, bloody-cornet-explosion scene. But, maybe that’s my shortcoming. Maybe my new allergy just reveals a bias of age, or temperament. Can I judge? This book has the rawness of youth. Did I leave too much piss &amp; vinegar at the door of middle-age? Do we get wise and ironical over the years, or just old and stale?<br /> <br />I would love to ask Ondaatje himself how he now finds the most dramatic parts of this first novel of his, published in 1975. <br /> <br />Interestingly, his 2007 novel Divisadero eschews ideas of ‘tortured genius’ and emphasises a contrary vision of the artist and creativity. “You are given a trade, not a gift. There need not be intensity or darkness in the service of it all.”<br /> <br />Contrast these killer passages from Coming Through Slaughter.<br /> <br />(Bolden criticising)<br />“Carey’s Trumpet was a technician – which went gliding down river and missed all the shit at the bottom.” <br /> <br />(Bolden describing those who inspired him)<br />“Don’t know what they taught me for the real teachers never teach you craft. […]It was good when you listened to Galloway bubble underneath the others and come through slipping and squealing into neighbourhoods that had nothing to do with the thumper tunes coming out of the rest.” <br /> <br />I love this description. And I love hearing this in music.<br />Monk<br />Sidney Bechet<br />Coltrane<br />Roland Kirk<br />Eric. Fucking. Dolphy. <br />And certain contemporary musicians in my own Toronto scene too. They’re all akin to Bolden in this way. It’s a jazz thing. It comes from an aversion to cliche, and an instinct for innovation, individualism of expression, and the sound of surprise. Ondaatje is trying to write the way these musicians play. He’s reaching for words and forms the way they reach for sounds. Poets are attuned to the sound of words, and Ondaatje is obviously uncomfortable with the old “thumpen tunes” of identifiable literary genres.<br /> <br /> <br />OKAY. So much of Coming Through Slaughter is awesome.<br /> <br />I love the package! (I have the original 1976 publication) The cover features a totally compelling, old photograph of Bolden posing with his band. It’s a damaged, blurry window onto a vanished world. After you pry your eyes off the cover and finally open the book, you encounter a second unusual image. It’s a sonograph of dolphin calls. It acts as a secondary visual epigraph, and metaphorical key to the book. “No one knows how a dolphin makes whistles and echolocation clicks simultaneously.”<br /> <br />I love how the dialogue doesn’t have quotation marks. The speakers are easily inferred, and everything flows with poetic concision. Who did this first? Cormac McCarthy? Ondaatje is really good at it.<br /> <br />Another thing I love will take a few pages to explain. If you’d rather skip this and finish the review, just scan forward to the asterisk.<br /> <br />This is a totally slick and unique thing that Ondaatje does in many of his books. He makes these calculatedly oblique jumps between sentences or paragraphs. It’s a sneaky move that knocks the reader off balance, and effects a sense of compression or expansion of time. He does it for various reasons.<br /> <br />For example, in this scene, Webb, a detective, is searching for his old friend Buddy who has disappeared from town. Webb is visiting and consulting with Buddy’s vulnerable and seductive wife Nora. Webb is of rare, detective Cooper’esque integrity. He is trying to extract himself from this suddenly sexually charged meeting in which he insists to her that Buddy will be back soon because Buddy is the one who needs and depends on her.<br /> <br />“Nora closing the door, narrow, just to the width of her face. Webb grins encouragement and walks slowly backwards down the four steps to the pavement. He has remembered the number of steps. He is wrong. Buddy will take two more years before he cruises home.”<br /> <br />So much is subtly happening here in those last three tension-snapping sentences! The middle one ambiguously refers to both the first and third. <br /> <br />This second sentence initially jars the reader with a visceral flight-or-fight response to the image of the good Webb falling backwards over a non-existent step that he thought was there.<br /> <br />The third sentence immediately subverts that interpretation while metaphorically carrying over the sensation of falling to the idea of one’s expectations and efforts being continually disappointed over time by life’s events. <br /> <br />The existentially loaded image of someone stepping backwards into air rather than onto concrete is perfectly calibrated for the themes of disappearance and obliteration that run through the book. <br /> <br />I remember Ondaatje did something similar in Divisadero using a jump between two paragraphs. The first, if I recall correctly, describes a character passing out. The second describes her coming to. A chronological and geographical gulf exists between the two paragraphs, but there is no warning or indication of this by the usual extra spacing or new chapter. The effect was at once jarring, disorienting, and a commentary on the doggedly persistent nature of trauma underlying that character’s lifetime. <br /> <br />Ondaatje messes with the reader’s perception of time in the same way that raw emotion and extreme experiences do. I’ve never encountered this kind of potent, poetic device with any other writer. <br /> <br /> <br /> <br /> <br />*<br /> <br />I also love Coming Through Slaughter’s collage of perspectives. <br /> <br />Like a cinematic flash-forward, the novel begins and ends with a present-tense, (revealed to be the mid 1970’s) first person narration that is implicitly the voice of the author as he drives around New Orleans. The narrator describes the dead silence and peeling paint of Buddy Bolden’s old neighbourhood, where no one has heard of that name. He goes on to intimate with his historical knowledge a vanished world of music, gambling and prostitution. The names of streets and rough &amp; colourful characters of the legendary Storyville-era New Orleans are described. The stage is set for Ondaatje’s imaginative dive into the times of Buddy Bolden. Ondaatje will play fast and loose with his protagonist and setting using them as a springboard towards an idiosyncratic portrait of artistic creation and madness. <br /> <br /><br /><br />At one point in the book Ondaatje describes historical knowledge as a “desert of facts”. The novel is centered on three important ones - Bolden’s musical genius, his sudden disappearance, and his public mental breakdown in a parade after reappearing on the scene. Ondaatje uses various first and third person narrators, stream of consciousness, as well as real and invented testimonies by Bolden’s contemporaries to breathe life into the facts.<br /> <br />The legendary and mythical figure of Buddy Bolden is the perfect ticket for Ondaatje’s freedom and poetic licence. All that remains of the unrecorded Bolden is the partially corroded photograph displayed on the cover, and a few recorded aural testimonies from greats of the ragtime era. They speak of his innovative bending and overblowing of notes, and his subversion of square marching rhythms with his syncopated ‘big four’ beat. Bolden is considered to be one of the first and iconic creators of early, hot jazz music in the way in which his loud, vulgar and freewheeling improvising blended the blues and ragtime with the sacred black music of the church.<br /> <br />Ondaatje imaginatively evokes Bolden’s sound using his multiple, fictional voices.<br /> <br />“It was a music that had so little wisdom that you wanted to clean every note that passed[…]It is good you never heard him play on recordings. If you never heard him play some place where the weather for instance could change the next series of notes - then you should never have heard him at all.[…]He stayed away while others moved into wax history, electronic history…”<br /> <br />“…and you like a weatherbird arcing around in the middle of your life to exact opposites and burning your brains out till[…]you were dropped into amber in the East Louisiana State Hospital. Some saying you went mad trying to play the devil’s music and hymns at the same time.”<br /> <br />“The right (song) ending is an open door you can’t see too far out of. It can mean the exact opposite of what you are thinking.”<br /> <br />The various narrators of the first part of the book circumambulate the enigmatic Bolden, so that it is with relish and excitement that we are suddenly dropped into his first person, inner-monologue-narration of his work-day, cutting hair at the barber shop while absentmindedly musing about mutilation of the face or neck under his razor!<br /> <br />Bolden’s inner world is slowly revealed to be a tension filled swirl of contradictory impulses. His almost child-like unreflectiveness and extroverted immersion in society, sex, gossip, and hard partying, is shadowed by violent urges to be free of all that, and a need to hide just like the wonderfully evoked “Water Turkey”. (pg. 27)<br /> <br />The character Bellocq is introduced and revealed to be a catalyst for Bolden’s withdrawal and unhinging mind, and at the same time a friend. <br /> <br />E. J. Bellocq, photographer, and hydrocephalic cripple is also an historical figure. He was another perfectly strange and dark Storyville character. Search him on Wiki. Ondaatje poetically embellishes Bellocq and weaves him into the narratives. He is an introverted, cerebral foil to Bolden. They are opposites that collide. They seem to me to be symbolic of two tethered poles of artistic creativity. Bolden is impulse, improvisation, ephemerality, unfiltered vitality and emotion. Bellocq is rigid composition and critical detachment. He freezes time and locks his subjects into black and white, two dimensional photos. Better to think of them as windows. Shattered windows, and corroded or burning photos are a leitmotif throughout Coming Through Slaughter.<br /> <br />Interestingly, it isn’t until after befriending Bellocq that thoughts like this enter Bolden’s mind – Bolden, who had always been happily immersed in the circulating love, sex and scandal of Storyville.<br /> <br />“Nora and Picket and me. Robin and Jaelin and me. I saw an awful thing among us. And that was passion could twist around and choose someone else just like that.”<br /> <br />Bolden defensively tries to describe Bellocq to Webb.<br /> <br />“…what I loved were the possibilities in his silence. He was just there like a small noon shadow…he didn’t rely on anything. He trusted nothing…he offered mole comfort, mole deceit.”<br /> <br />Ondaatje’s Bellocq is fascinating, and another of my favourite things about Coming Through Slaughter. He is an outsider in a city of outsiders. He’s twisted. He photographs local prostitutes for his private collection. But, he does so with their consent, and the portraits are strangely gentle and even humane windows onto a dark world. <br /> <br />These photos are not Ondaatje’s invention. They exist in real life! They probably account for Bellocq’s Wikipedia status. The glass plate negatives were found after his death, and sold to a photographer who posthumously printed them. <br /> <br />I didn’t know any of this until years after I read Coming Through Slaughter when they appeared at an art gallery down the street from me. They are remarkable – disturbing, but aesthetically beautiful. You can now purchase them in the book “Storyville Portraits”. It has a forward by Susan Sontag! It looks expensive though. Plus, you might not want your in-laws curiously plucking it from your shelf. Just view the photos online. You can read the Sontag here. <br /> <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.independent.co.uk/arts-entertainment/sinful-flesh-1334956.html">https://www.independent.co.uk/arts-en...</a><br /> <br />Ondaatje’s fusing of fact and fiction is just another of the things that makes Coming Through Slaughter so great. If there was ever a place and time in which truth was stranger than fiction, ragtime-era New Orleans was it. This grants Ondaatje so much freedom to invent and weave while remaining plausible. His fictional truths and the historical truths amplify each other. <br /> <br />In the final pages, he weaves fictional passages with biographical documents about Bolden, Louisiana State Hospital records, and excerpts from interviews. This “desert of facts” hits the reader like a shockwave after the novel’s climactic scene.<br /> <br />For the final paragraph of the book, Ondaatje takes the reader inside the hospital, and back inside Bolden’s mind. The last few sentences are diamonds.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2872713770913471492" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/678506-patrick-mccoy?shelf=contemporary-fiction">contemporary-fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1731503686" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15086946514464174501">I decided to read Michael Ondaajte's jazz age novel, Coming Through Slaughter (1976), as background reading to color in my recent trip to New Orleans. And color it did provide: sex, love triangles, gambling, drinking, whoring, music and a tour of the now lost Storyville red light district. This novel is a fictional depiction of the life of an obscure jazz musician, coronet player Buddy Bolden. It is experimental in form and has a lyrical quality that sets it apart from other novels in that much </span>
  <span id="freeText15086946514464174501" style="display:none">I decided to read Michael Ondaajte's jazz age novel, Coming Through Slaughter (1976), as background reading to color in my recent trip to New Orleans. And color it did provide: sex, love triangles, gambling, drinking, whoring, music and a tour of the now lost Storyville red light district. This novel is a fictional depiction of the life of an obscure jazz musician, coronet player Buddy Bolden. It is experimental in form and has a lyrical quality that sets it apart from other novels in that much of the impact of the novel comes from the impressions and atmosphere of the story rather than the plot which is less than linear. It does seem that this early jazz pioneer had a breakdown that landed him in an insane asylum, which tells us that we would really never know what it was that landed him in that institution but ripe for the imagination of a writer like Ondaajte.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15086946514464174501" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2886057704" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2886057704" />
    <a title="Mirnes Alispahić" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43368601-mirnes-alispahi"><img alt="Mirnes Alispahić" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1500813640p2/43368601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2886057704?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mirnes Alispahić" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mirnes Alispahić" href="/user/show/43368601-mirnes-alispahi">Mirnes Alispahić</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2886057704" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10809039743701177752">When it comes to wordplay and style, Michael Ondaatje is a mage of old. There is no doubt about it. The magic of his writing is extraordinary; it stirs emotions and evokes memories, some not even belong to us, yet they come alive. 'Coming Through Slaughter' is his debut novel, though he was already an established poet by that time. If you've read his poetry, then this novel might not be too experimental for you because it can be viewed as a continuation of his poetry, so vivid and compelling in </span>
  <span id="freeText10809039743701177752" style="display:none">When it comes to wordplay and style, Michael Ondaatje is a mage of old. There is no doubt about it. The magic of his writing is extraordinary; it stirs emotions and evokes memories, some not even belong to us, yet they come alive. 'Coming Through Slaughter' is his debut novel, though he was already an established poet by that time. If you've read his poetry, then this novel might not be too experimental for you because it can be viewed as a continuation of his poetry, so vivid and compelling in certain scenes that the atmosphere is palpable. <br />In 'Coming Through Slaughter' Ondaatje writes of Buddy Bolden, a legendary jazz musician who had a mental breakdown at the age of 31 in the middle of Mardi Gras parade, after which he went to the asylum never to return again. Playing with style and narrative, facts, and rumours, Ondaatje blurs the lines between the real Bolden and fictional. Thus he presents us a fragmented tale of self-destructive mad musical genius Buddy Bolden, who is torn between love for two women, his wife and mistress. There is also an introvert photographer who takes pictures of New Orleans Red District prostitutes, and a policeman who is a friend of Buddy. Ondaatje is there as well, as an occasional narrator who shares his thoughts with readers during his writing and researching. <br />Not many writers can do what Ondaatje so masterfully does, to adjust the style to the narrative and his desires. The tale of 'Coming Through Slaughter' is like a free jazz tune, improvised as it plays along. A writer writes by his feeling. Erratic thoughts. Disembodied dialogues. News clippings. Interviews. Letters. All of this is put together in this experimental form of a novel, which will put away many readers. Yet, if you're a fan of Ondaatje's writing, this is a book not to be missed. A fair warning, though, do not expect a classic novel, for you will not get it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10809039743701177752" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Rhys" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1138007-rhys"><img alt="Rhys" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442456315p2/1138007.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2305816941?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rhys" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rhys" href="/user/show/1138007-rhys">Rhys</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2305816941" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1585559340209294856">The first Ondaatje novel I have read and on the strength of this, I will certainly be seeking out his other books. This is the imagined story of Buddy Bolden, the jazz pioneer about whom very little is actually known, and the prose is exquisite, original, clever and frequently offbeat, just like the best jazz music. At times I was reminded of one of the absurd and highly inventive 'routines' of a William Burroughs novel, for example the incident of the woman who is strangled Isadora Duncan style</span>
  <span id="freeText1585559340209294856" style="display:none">The first Ondaatje novel I have read and on the strength of this, I will certainly be seeking out his other books. This is the imagined story of Buddy Bolden, the jazz pioneer about whom very little is actually known, and the prose is exquisite, original, clever and frequently offbeat, just like the best jazz music. At times I was reminded of one of the absurd and highly inventive 'routines' of a William Burroughs novel, for example the incident of the woman who is strangled Isadora Duncan style by her pet snake while she is driving. The last quarter of the book flagged a little and I don't know why. Yet I am grateful I picked it up at random in a charity shop last weekend and have now finished it.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('aCRBxDvYkcN90zX1xFbtBjB+2YOB6wdfQcskldRR9/8l9GhVDXyzgNc6+ONBcELOTcmdp/Ud+kDbXtBqR3UNtQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('426py67iX/uyzIy8G+QTJoDeWbdxj+c29p3ArV1VNKKuvoBamEZ9uBglQaqewrzu/WkdkwV5GilsCDRSznHO6A==')})
  }

In the center of the collection is the title poem, &quot;Voyage of the Sable Venus,&quot; an amazing narrative made up entirely of titles of artworks from ancient times to the present--titles that feature or in some way comment on the black female figure in Western art.\n\nBracketed by Lewis\'s own autobiographical poems, Voyage is a tender and shocking meditation on the fragmentary mysteries of stereotype, juxtaposing our names for things with what we actually see and know. A new understanding of biography and the self, this collection questions just where, historically, do ideas about the black female figure truly begin--five hundred years ago, five thousand, or even longer? And what role did art play in this ancient, often heinous story?\n\nHere we meet a poet who adores her culture and the beauty to be found within it. Yet she is also a cultural critic alert to the nuances of race and desire--how they define us all, including her own sometimes painful history. Lewis\'s book is a thrilling aesthetic anthem to the complexity of race--a full embrace of its pleasure and horror, in equal parts.From the Hardcover edition.<\/span>\n  <a He quickl<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText11346725942799953134\" style=\"display:none\">The hidden history of a haunted and beloved city told through the intersecting lives of nine remarkable characters After Hurricane Katrina, Dan Baum moved to New Orleans to write about the city’s response to the disaster for The New Yorker. He quickly realized that Katrina was not the most interesting thing about New Orleans, not by a long shot. The most interesting question, which struck him as he watched residents struggling to return, was this: Why are New Orleanians—along with people from all over the world who continue to flock there—so devoted to a place that was, even before the storm, the most corrupt, impoverished, and violent corner of America?\n\nHere’s the answer. Nine Lives is a multivoiced biography of this dazzling, surreal, and imperiled city through the lives of nine characters over forty years and bracketed by two epic storms: Hurricane Betsy, which transformed the city in the 1960’s, and Katrina, which nearly destroyed it. These nine lives are windows into every strata of one of the most complex and fascinating cities in the world. From outsider artists and Mardi Gras Kings to jazz-playing coroners and transsexual barkeeps, these lives are possible only in New Orleans, but the city that nurtures them is also, from the beginning, a city haunted by the possibility of disaster. All their stories converge in the storm, where some characters rise to acts of heroism and others sink to the bottom. But it is New Orleans herself—perpetually whistling past the grave yard—that is the story’s real heroine. \n\nNine Lives is narrated from the points of view of some of New Orleans’s most charismatic characters, but underpinning the voices of the city is an extraordinary feat of reporting that allows Baum to bring this kaleidoscopic portrait to life with brilliant color and crystalline detail. Readers will find themselves wrapped up in each of these individual dramas and delightfully immersed in the life of one of this country’s last unique places, even as its ultimate devastation looms ever closer. By resurrecting this beautiful and tragic place and portraying the extraordinary lives that could have taken root only there, Nine Lives shows us what was lost in the storm and what remains to be Though she can barely see above his head, she holds forth about her life and desires, her struggles with her sexuality and identity. Born and raised in Germany, she has been living in London for several years, determined to break free from her family origins and her haunted homeland. But the recent death of her grandfather, and an unexpected inheritance, make it clear that you cannot easily outrun your own shame, whether it be physical, familial, historical, national, or all of the above. Though he lasted only a few months before illness and personal crisis forced him home, Bissell found himself entranced by this remote land. Five years later he returned to explore the shrinking Aral Sea, destroyed by Soviet irrigation policies. Joining up with an exuberant translator named Rustam, Bissell slips more than once through the clutches of the Uzbek police as he makes his often wild way to the devastated sea.In Chasing the Sea,  Bissell combines the story of his travels with a beguiling chronicle of Uzbekistan’s striking culture and long history of violent subjugation by despots from Jenghiz Khan to Joseph Stalin.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4030.Michael_Ondaatje">About Michael Ondaatje</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4030.Michael_Ondaatje"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1390116915p3/4030.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4030.Michael_Ondaatje">Michael Ondaatje
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
3,014
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer224514928200926750">He was born to a Burgher family of Dutch-Tamil-Sinhalese-Portuguese origin. He moved to England with his mother in 1954. After relocating to Canada in 1962, Ondaatje became a Canadian citizen. Ondaatje studied for a time at Bishops College School and Bishop's University in Lennoxville, Quebec, but moved to Toronto and received his BA from the University of Toronto and his MA from Queen's Universit</span>
  <span id="freeText224514928200926750" style="display:none">He was born to a Burgher family of Dutch-Tamil-Sinhalese-Portuguese origin. He moved to England with his mother in 1954. After relocating to Canada in 1962, Ondaatje became a Canadian citizen. Ondaatje studied for a time at Bishops College School and Bishop's University in Lennoxville, Quebec, but moved to Toronto and received his BA from the University of Toronto and his MA from Queen's University in Kingston, Ontario and began teaching at the University of Western Ontario in London, Ontario. In 1970 he settled in Toronto. From 1971 to 1988 he taught English Literature at York University and Glendon College in Toronto.<br /><br />He and his wife, novelist and academic Linda Spalding, co-edit Brick, A Literary Journal, with Michael Redhill, Michael Helm, and Esta Spalding.<br /><br />Although he is best known as a novelist, Ondaatje's work also encompasses memoir, poetry, and film.<br /><br />Ondaatje has, since the 1960s, also been involved with Toronto's influential Coach House Books, supporting the independent small press by working as a poetry editor.<br /><br />In 1988 Michael Ondaatje was made an Officer of the Order of Canada (OC) and two years later became a Foreign Honorary Member of the American Academy of Arts and Letters.<br /><br />He has two children and is the brother of philanthropist, businessman, and author Christopher Ondaatje.<br /><br />In 1992 he received the Man Booker Prize for his winning novel adapted into an Academy-Award-winning film, <i>The English Patient</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="224514928200926750" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
